0385534639|4.0|The Night Circus is purely magical. Opening its covers is like stepping into a dream from which you will never want to wake; every element of the story is almost painfully beautiful. If I were dropped on a desert island with only a single box of books, this book would be in that box.  The Night Circus is the story of Celia and Marco, a girl and a boy chosen and trained by two different magicians to compete in a game of illusion and strength. The game commences when they are adults, and it is played in a dream-like circus that travels the world. One illusionist travels with the circus while the other plays from afar. They fall in love, neither of them realizing that the game will only end when one of them dies. The circus impacts more and more lives the longer it goes on, and not always in a good way. Alongside the interplay of Celia and Marco is the story of Bailey, a boy fascinated by the circus who will play an integral role as the two story lines merge.  When I closed the book and the dream ended, I started to realize that the book wasn't perfect. The characters could have been developed further, (I feel like the defining characteristics of the main characters were quite similar), I would have chosen a completely different ending, and yes, almost every author would benefit from a good editor. However, those things don't stop me from loving the book for what it is. Erin Morgenstern's setting is enchanting, and her story is gripping. I can't wait to see what she writes next!

Hardware and software wise, the product itself is perfect as a functional and easy to use e-Reader. I am aware the the Nook supports e-pub which allows it to read generic pdf files (something that the Kindle can't do), but one of the features I look for in e-readers is convenience. Having to buy a book from Google books on my laptop and then transferring it to my Nook is not convenient. If only this device had the Kindle store on it...  Bottom line: When you buy an e-reader, you're also buying into the manufacturer's entire ecosystem. With the Kindle, you're buying material from an expanding and reliable retailer that's not going away anytime soon. With the Nook, you're buying material from a reputable but inconsistent company whose mettle has yet to be tested in the e-reader space.   Two word recommendation: Research selection (before purchase)  I hope I've helped.

0634049011|5.0|This book is nothing short of amazing!!!<br />I have been playing guitar for 20 years this month, and never took the time to learn much of the theory as it was always too much to memorize...<br />Firstly, I recommend this book to the novice who just wants to learn a few songs just to impress some ladies - on up to the professional guitarists making a living on their 6 stringed' friend.<br />Beginners: Don't be threatened. The first chapter is designed to get you familiar with the guitar. This book promises to make accomplished guitarists out of people first learning guitar within a year, and I believe with the discipline ANYONE can do it.<br />Intermediate: This may be your next step to becoming one of the greats. This would have set me way above the rest of the students in all school and garage bands at my age.<br />Advanced: (I consider myself to be between this and the next) It's not too late! You will make light work of this and jump leaps and bounds from where you left off. It will probably be most exciting for you.<br />Professionals: You will already know a lot of this. This will help you fly through the book. By showing you shortcuts, you can access more notes, faster than you're able to drudge up from memorized scales and repetitive riffs. And of course one of the great things about being a good guitarist is being able to teach right!?<br />This can be the ultimate workbook for your students. They will, (with your help) learn quickly too, and you may be able to expand your \\"skill range\\" of students you're able to teach. And your reputation will reflect. If you're anything like me, I can always use help with being more creative soloing and this book does that for me too.<br /><br />This book has ways, only for the guitar, to get around a lot of the boring parts of theory that you have to memorize. For example, instead of learning what note every fret is on the board one by one (which is important if you want to play professionally), this workbook shows you five simple patterns to memorize. You build on these patterns in each chapter until you can play any mode of any scale from anywhere on your guitar! You will automatically become familiar with the names and be able to figure out what every note is if you haven't yet memorized that the 2nd string 11th fret is a B flat.<br />You'll learn everything you need to know about the fret board just by having fun with these root shapes and patterns.<br />I learned to play by ear and over the first few years, I had been forced to memorize some of these root shapes, so I already knew somewhat of the importance of these patterns. I can and have jammed with some great musicians over the years, but one thing I felt I couldn't do very well was solo, unless I was familiar with the key and had memorized riffs I could slip into variations of the melody to sound good, and even then, I'll still hit an accidental and sound just as good as my one year old! I never took the time to memorize but a handful of scales; never enough to improvise or be creative with my solos.<br />By getting familiar with these patterns and learning how they connect to each other and can be played everywhere, I found they would be the foundation to every chord, arpeggio, and scale that I did know and ever wanted to know!!<br />You won't regret this creative way of looking at the relationships between the scales and arpeggios, and in a light funny way of presenting it to you. The book uses hands on, verbal exercises, and plenty of diagrams to show and explain to every kind of learner what exactly is happening in each chapter. Highly recommend!!!!<br />Rob - Dadgigsopics

The present day world is where we begin and it's a place of danger and impending doom. The setting and the characters are intriguing. All I know is that my great headphones sound even a little more great, whether because the sound is actually augmented (which I am beginning to believe) or simply because - fool that I am - I am just listening to them slightly louder.<br /><br />This will drive my headphones much louder than they can play on their own - to head banging levels - but that is not important to me as I never listen at max volume even without the Fiio's help.<br /><br />Unit is very small (about the size of an iPod Nano) and seems quite well made.  So far I have not found it too awkward adding another link between the player and the headphones, but I probably look a bit more geeky than usual if I don't take the trouble to try to hide all this gadgetry (the heavy cable of the Grados is problematic enough).<br /><br />I do listen through this all the time now, but since it's bought and paid for why wouldn't I?  Know what I mean?

0743289692|5.0|If you've ever wondered what the Western World went through for several hundred years to get to the enlightened freedoms we know today, Ayaan Hirsi Ali's memoir is as good a glimpse as I've seen outside of my own emancipation, & that's what my husband said when he gave me secondhand hardback copies of INFIDEL & NOMAD for Christmas... way to go, Hubby, & thanks ;-}<br /><br />Ayaan Hirsi Ali tells of her life in moments that fractured her soul, administered by the men of her culture, & the subjugated & emotionally feral women in her family ~ all intent on breaking her spirit into submission cuz that's what the holy book of her father's religion says they must do to their females.<br /><br />By way of introduction she writes of the fracture we all saw recently on the news: the assassination in Holland of Theo van Gogh by a Muslim immigrant who shot the movie director on the street in broad daylight, slit his throat & then stabbed a fatwa paper onto his dead chest. Theo & Ayaan had made that brief movie SUBMISSION Part 1, wherein certain Quranic verses are seen floating over the shadowed undressed body of a woman.<br /><br />After repeating the generations of her father's family, as Somalis are wont to do to identify tribal, clan & subclan roots & perhaps find family ties, she tells of how her parents met & how her mother chose her husband which was, as you will see, highly unusual. Then come the blissful years of monkey life under the Talal tree with her mother's mother, her older brother & baby sister outside her home.<br /><br />Now that you're thoroughly lulled comes the first betrayal, the first act of terror: your grandmother, she of all desert wisdom, brings a strange man into your home, then she & two neighbors trap your older brother, then you & then your sister on the floor & he attacks you \\"down there\\" with a knife. Then he sews you up, leaving a minute hole for your urine to drain. Grandmother tosses you on your bed with your legs bound & forbids you to move. You are six years of age.<br /><br />Of course Ayaan's brother is merely circumcised whereas she & her sister have only a scar now: no labias nor clitoris. They will know only agonizingly traumatic sexual intercourse (apparently lovemaking between husbands & their wives is not expected, only varying degrees of rape) & must have surgery to reopen them to give birth, thus many first time mothers die as do many girls from infection, filthy tools & conditions immediately after. I came across female genital mutilation back in the '80s when I worked for an alternative medical magazine & published an article by an ER doctor who'd cared for such a patient & wanted to know more about it: were any US laws about this horrifyingly unnecessary excision & did the people performing them have a license. I gathered it's more a cultural thing than Islamic as it's not mentioned in the Quran, however, beating your wife is & that happens with sickening regularity, as Ayaan attests to of neighbors back in the Old Countries & in European immigrant housing: the lines between culture & religion are often blurred.<br /><br />Now add her father's political bent & all of a sudden they must flee to Saudi Arabia, the most Islamic & repressive nation in the world, North Korean notwithstanding! Without her mother there to help & having to do everything on her own with no man around to accompany her outside, Ayaan's mother begins to degenerate, taking out her frustrations & furies on her oldest daughter who already is doing all the housework. Now the mother administers daily beatings even as she finds the purest experience of her religion in a nation of men who despise & cloister all things female.<br /><br />Our heroine grows a-pace & their father moves them to a large Somali enclave in Nairobi, Kenya where she starts school, is fascinated & inquiring which is not so good cuz that's not how Islam is taught, especially to girls. There is no dialogue, no questioning, only total by rote submissionto the lesson. Somewhere in there a teacher is hired & so begins a struggle of dominance which he ends by beating the you-know-what out of his student, breaking her skull. After hospitalization, she survives, except numbers will forever be difficult for her to grasp.<br /><br />Each page of INFIDEL pummeled me awake: I could not get enough & kept wondering what would tip her over the edge. How would this tall, exquisite jewel of a woman get out of the culture that was bound & determined to break her into the Islamic version of a Stepford Wife.<br /><br />This is one riveting coming of age & coming out story. I was howling & cheering, furious & elated as Ayaan Hirsi Ali told her stories from first memories, into teenhood & the devout faith Sister Aziza inspired, to her troubled baby sister, to her cliche of a brother, to her parents' separation, to her mother's breakdown, to her father's arranged marriage for her to a distant relative in Canada, to her sojourn in Holland & her education & enlightenment, her stint as a Member of Parliament to meeting Theo van Gogh, to death threats & Dutch bureaucratic rejection of her citizenship, to eventual escape to America.<br /><br />A word about when she first arrived in Europe: she finds everything remarkable & gushes over how clean the cities were, how miraculous indoor plumbing is & how magical were the buses that arrived when the timetable said they would. Her insights are not only revealing of where she came from, they are often amusing!<br /><br />How her politics about immigration evolved after learning the language (she already knew English from living in Kenya), who she met both as a refugee & student in the land of Enlightenment; the work she took on: cleaning, typing, translating & living wherever she could find a place away from immigrant ghettos. She used the system already in existence modestly with the intent of spring boarding to a life she could support with her own earnings: exactly the opposite to the immigrants who live off such generous welfare payments that they have no need to seek work, while the women are still forbidden to venture out into the new land, may not learn the language nor attain citizenship.<br /><br />INFIDEL is a very well written keeper: flowing language, lucid descriptions, asking questions without giving away the answers. You should see all my \\"Ahha!\\" Moments I made in the margins ~ a no-brainer for Women's Book Clubs & a textbook for psychology students... WOW!truggle of dominance which he ends by beating the you-know-what out of his student, breaking her skull. B0000073BG|5.0|NOTHING could have prepared me for the excellence found throughout Scheherazade & Other Stories.  Also, nothing could have prepared me for the difficulty in pronouncing the word \\"Scheherazade\\", haha!  Do I love this album from beginning to end?  Do I ever!  Looking for a weak point would take me an eternity to find, because if there is one, I honestly have trouble spotting it.    The thing that really escalates this album above the other ones, in my opinion at least, is the way Annie Haslam really feels like she's locked in this time around.  Her voice has always been fantastic, but something about her vocal melodies on this album just leaves me speechless.    \\"Trip To the Fair\\" is amazing on some indescribable level.  It sounds almost like a nursery rhyme in spots, thanks to the gentle way the piano sails along.  The classical-like complexity of the 3-minute intro makes it seem more sophisticated than it would initially seem.  From here... it just blows me away.  Annie's vocals come in, and this might be the catchiest vocal melody I've ever heard on a Renaissance album.  \\"A trip to the fair... but nobody was there!\\"  Annie sings this line in such a realistic way that it makes you feel abandoned almost, like you're really there.  I can just imagine taking a trip alone to a fair, arriving at night and... the only thing there is a locked gate, turned off lights and the sound of the wind blowing.  Just a phenomenal song.  \\"Vultures Fly High\\" would have made a wonderful pop single.  It contains another fantastic vocal melody, but with a fast pace (well, faster than most Renaissance songs).  It might not be the flashiest song on the album, but it proves Renaissance had something doing good here.  \\"Ocean Gypsy\\" has some rather poetic and inspiring lyrics, and quality vocals as well.  The piano solo in the middle is truly something else.  It almost reminds me of the Genesis classic \\"Firth of Fifth\\" but it's not quite the same.  Alright, these lyrics probably don't appeal to me a whole lot since it's basically romantic poetry (at least, it sure seems like it) but I don't care.  But then... then... THEN comes the 24-minute minutes of perfection to end this album.  Speaking of Genesis, I might even put this 24-minute track above \\"Supper's Ready\\" and that's not necessarily an easy task for me since \\"Supper's Ready\\" was always a high point for me (mainly because it was the first prog epic I've ever experienced, so the memories are precious and unforgettable) but *oh my freaking goodness* does \\"Song of Scheherazade\\" do everything right.  For 24 straight minutes, all the arrangements are put together in just the right way, Annie's voice builds and soars in all the appropriate places, and it's just a perfect song to me.  I can see myself listening to this song over and over on headphones all night long... because that's actually what I did last night!    If you even think for a moment you want to be a progressive rock fan, I HIGHLY recommend this album.  It's nothing short of brilliant.

B004AYCWY4|5.0|THE GREATEST SHOW ON EARTH, by retired science professor Richard Dawkins of Oxford University, is at once of `thriller' and 'sure-footed non-fiction' genres. Mind-bending to wondrously eye-opening, this work clarifies the mountainous evidence for Evolution. How nice the synchronicity: publishing it on the 200th anniversary of Charles Darwin's birth and the 150th of The Origin of Species. Marketing has its place. I had mulled Darwin's statement: \\"There is grandeur in this view of life.\\" After reading TGSOE, it rang truer yet. And toward the last of its 437 pages, I was almost giddy (sounds isotopily unstable, I know)<br /><br />[Footnote: The Bible and Qur'an are not science books] Scientific findings should not be ignored, dismissed out of hand, or its data unanalyzed, because it belies dogma; often enough, human animals are seduced into delusional world views due to fear; perusing a few pages here n there just might start a process of freeing one up, however, from its limiting effects. When is it that one gets dissuaded from questioning? Or derided for critical thinking? Or if fortunate, encouraged to curiosity? Well, if YOU were dissuaded at some point, Dawkins' book may be your ticket to a life-renewing antidote. (Consider visiting a museum of natural history, too.) Now I doubt that the mere reading of this work will result in all the intellectual mutations Dawkins hoped for; certainly not among the devout, who faithfully remain cocooned in the beguiling comforts of  platitudes. But in time...<br /><br />Revelation: 1) modern species do not evolve into other modern species; they share common ancestors. 2) Animal and plant breeding--techniques applied for centuries--give us insight into branches of living diversity that spread over millions upon millions of years, via random, mutating genes within DNA. (Broccoli, cauliflower, kale, Brussels sprouts have all  derived--via non-random/artificial selection by bright humans--from a common ancestor: wild cabbage) Pedigree dog-breeding led to today's 200+ breeds, each sharing a common ancestor: the wolf; molecular genetics proved it. Wolves adapted to man via natural selection, by self-domesticating into village dogs. 3) When we observe an increase or decrease in the frequency with which a certain gene appears in a gene pool, THAT is evolution. 4) Skilful breeding (artificial, or by insects, bats, hummingbirds, frogs) of roses or sunflowers, produced innumerable varieties. 5) Cross-fertilization via pollination led Darwin, who'd come upon an orchid with a foot-long tubular, to predict the existence of a very long-nosed Madagascar moth with an even longer tongue; just such a moth was found. 6) By sexual selection, Nature plays the role of a selecting agent, and living bodies are patchworks of compromise aimed at survival, but hardly perfectly. 7) Wild foxes have been bred for tameness, using variations in `flight distances' and cute facial expressions. 8) Orchids exist that fool male bees into pollinating them by looking like a female bee. 9) Measured age of Earth is 46 million centuries--4.6 billion years. Now let that sink in. Hmm?<br /><br />Measuring Time: re: Radioactive clocks--unstable isotopes decay, turning into something else at known rates; `half-life' is a favored measure: time for half an element's atoms to decay; Geological clocks--tree rings (date back 11,500 years), coral-reef rings, carbon dating, which began in the 1940s, was refined by mass spectrometry; an accumulating quantity of argon-40 in a rock crystal derives from decay in potassium-40, and these accumulations enable elapsed-time measurements. If an igneous rock shows a 50-50 ratio of argon to potassium, it is 1.26 million years old. Now at the moment an animal or plant dies it ceases to take in Carbon-14, and so begins to decay to Nitrogen-14; elapsed time can be measured as the ratio of carbon-12 to carbon-14 in the deceased. Is chemistry hot or cool? Maybe both.<br /><br />Evolution in our time: a) elephants' tusks shortening within mere decades, as humans killed for ivory; these mammals suspected or knew the reason why, at some level; b) evolutionary divergence was also evidenced on islands off Croatia: in 1971, pairs of lizards were introduced to a non-lizard islet; then in 2008--some 19 lizard generations later--a thriving population of `new-home' lizards was found to have grown larger heads than the `old home' lizards. The evolutionary change? Traced to the vegetarian diet in their new islet setting. c) Guppies (aquarium fish) evolve with blend-in camouflage--depending on type/prevalence of predators--or by changing color-schemes where predators are scarcer, all in an effort by males to attract females by standing out. (Hey, tattoos may yet become inked into our DNA)<br /><br />A fundamental (ist) myth whacked: No modern species is descended from any other modern species; nope, humans are NOT descended from monkeys; but each species shares a common ancestor that dates back 6 million years. `Lucy' from Ethiopia/1974, aka Australopithecus afarensis, is a bipedal hominid fossil 3.2 million years old. Homo erectus fossils (Java Man), less than a million years old, were unearthed in 1891; in 1929 (Peking Man). Ardi, found in Ethiopia in 1992, are the fossilized skeletal remains of a female Ardipithecus ramidus, a human-like species 4.4 million years old. And the bits just keep on comin'<br /><br />\\"Natural selection favors the survival in the gene pool of the genetic mutations responsible for making crucial changes in embryos.\\" Dawkins devotes Ch 9 to how geographical barriers (islands or isolation) play key roles in evolution; barriers arise via quakes, tsunamis, climate change, volcanic eruptions, continental drift. In Ch 10, he shows us a fascinating display of similarities--invariance across all vertebrates--among mammalian skeletons, despite differences in skeletal size, shape, or purpose (viz., Pterodactyl vs. human). The photography and illustrations are first-rate.<br /><br />The molecular clock assumes that aspects of evolution itself proceed at fixed rates. The genome is subject to mutations; only in a tiny portion of it, however, is it that any mutation bears on survival; natural selection rids deleterious mutations, yet favors ones that enhance life by forging adaptations to changing environments. In evolutionary time, mutations may go `to fixation'--become part of a genetic norm. Evolutionary history is written all over us. In dolphins, too--for having migrated from land to sea they still have lungs, not gills; nature's record is dotted with major flaws being \\"corrected\\"--a genetic tinkering, if you will, at a local level that nature required for a species' survival.<br /><br />Given contemporary human health woes, the Devil's chaplain could gleefully write a helluva book recounting them all, but Dawkins emphasizes that nature is neither kind nor unkind; neither favoring nor opposing suffering. Near the end of this book comes a hint of what Malthus, Darwin, and the like have already noted (as has non-notable me): human over-population has dire implications, ones we ought not to dismiss.<br /><br />Can't recommend this book enough. Sheer fun, and a worthwhile benefit: enrichment.decades, as humans killed for ivory; these mammals suspected or knew the reason why, at some level; b) evolutionary divergence was also evidenced on islands off Croatia: in 1971, pairs of lizards were introduced to a non-lizard islet; then in 2008--some 19 lizard generations later--a thriving population of `new-home' lizards was found to have grown larger heads than the `old home' lizards. B001JJX7L0|5.0|There is always a question about composers of the great pianoforte music as Chopin, Liszt, Beethoven, those whose playing was not there when the sound recording was - how would he play his own music?  How did Beethoven play \\"Waldstein\\" himself?  And how did Chopin played his own mazurkas or ballades?  Or at least, how would he wish it to be played?<br /><br />Listening to this recording, one comes to a conclusion that it simply does not get any better; or that this is probably how CHopin envisioned, or heard his music playing in his mind.  The indescribable elegance of Arturo Benedetti Michelangeli, coupled with such a sprezzatura technique that consequently you don't even notice it, you hear only the music, makes his playing miracle.  Another miracle is that he passed his art on another great pianist, Maurizio Pollini, who was his pupil, and at this point I think this Italian school is superior to my long-time worshiped titan Vladimir Horowitz.  While Horowitz is technically unsurpassed - yet Pollini is no less - the poetry of Michelangeli is far beyond, which he narrates in the Ballade in G minor, Op. 23.  One can easily compare the two interpretations:<br /><br />[[ASIN:B0013AYXNQ Horowitz Plays Chopin: Vol. 1]]<br /><br />The mazurkas by Michelangeli simply evoke the atmosphere of an aristocratic salon, perhaps that of Georges Sand where some of them were surely played - by the maestro and maybe occasionally by Franz Liszt? When one tries to invoke that refined yet quite libertine and appropriately romantic environment, the loves Georges Sand and Chopin, of Countess d'Agoult and Liszt, elegant and well-supplied by the ladies' riches, a truly unique pictures emerges; and that special mood of making one's life as a work of art pours from Michelangeli's interpretation of Chopin's music.  When in Berlin, it is interesting to contemplate on the subject in front of this picture in Alte Nationalgalerie - \\"Liszt at the Piano, 1840, Josef Danhauser\\" and it is easy to see Chopin in Liszt's place there, in such a quintessentially romantic pose, with his fair lady at his feet, looking up at the performing Orpheo in a state of awe, while other worshippers seem to freeze in adulation - they look like Schubert or Chateaubriand but in fact are Alexandre Dumas and Victor Hugo; the bust of Beethoven seems to smile from what he hears, and Lord Byron nods approvingly from his portrait...<br /><br />My favorite mazurka on this recording is Op.68 No.2 - Lento, for a simple reason that although it is so deceptively simple, written in C major, I can never play it correctly, and for a long time Michelangeli playing of this piece simply mesmerizes me.<br /><br />Yet it is unquestionably the ballade that is the most challenging, for both interpretive and technical reason.  I have heard a few interpretations of this op. 23, including live performances, but this one is above and beyond all of them, including Horowitz, as I mentioned.  How many more superlatives it is possible to give?  I want to celebrate this recording by concluding with the history of this ballade which is as fascinating as the musical piece itself; my foreword would be - is it not another pleasant fact regarding Chopin's genius that he did not seem to share the Gothic Teutonic taste for ballades, and his ballades always sound luminous and optimistic, even if they are full of mysterious whispers?<br /><br />Here is the story:<br /><br />A \\"ballad,\\" according to the Random House Dictionary, is \\"a simple, narrative poem of popular origin, composed in short stanzas, especially one of romantic character and adapted for singing.\\" The term was derived from an ancient musico-poetic form that accompanied dancing (\\"ballare\\" in medieval Latin, hence \\"ball\\" and \\"ballet\\"), which had evolved into an independent vocal genre by the 14th century in the exquisitely refined works of Guillaume de Machant and other early composers of secular music. The ballad was well established in England as a mediumfor the recitation of romantic or fantastic stories by at least the year 1500; it is mentioned by Pepys, Milton, Addison, and Swift, often disdainfully because of the frequently scurrilous nature of its content. The form, having adopted a more elegant demeanor, became popular in Germany during the late 18th century when it attracted no less a literary luminary than Goethe, whose tragic narrative Erlk&ouml;nig furnished the text for one of Schubert's most beloved songs.<br /><br />[[ASIN:B006W2H6IY Schubert Lieder Erlkonig Premium Poster Print, 24x32]]<br /><br />Chopin seems to have been the first composer to apply the title to a piece of abstract instrumental music, apparently indicating that his four Ballades hint at a dramatic flow of emotions such as could not be appropriately contained by traditional Classical forms. (Such transferral of terms between artistic disciplines was hardly unknown during the Romantic era. Liszt, the first musical artist in history with enough nerve to keep an entire public program to himself, dubbed his solo concerts \\"musical soliloquies\\" at first, and later gave them the now-familiar designation, \\"recitals.\\" --\\"How can one recite at the piano?\\" fumed one British critic. \\"Preposterous!\\"). Brahms, Liszt, Faur&eacute;, Grieg, Vieuxtemps, and Frank Martin all later provided instrumental works with the title Ballade.<br /><br />In the Ballades, \\"Chopin reaches his full stature as the unapproachable genius of the pianoforte,\\" according to Arthur Hedley, \\"a master of rich and subtle harmony and, above all, a poet--one of those whose vision transcends the confines of nation and epoch, and whose mission it is to share with the world some of the beauty that is revealed to them alone.\\" Though the Ballades came to form a nicely cohesive set unified by their temporal scale, structural fluidity, and supranational idiom, Chopin composed them over a period of more than a decade. He once suggested to Robert Schumann that he was \\"incited to the creation of the Ballades\\" by some poems of his Polish compatriot Adam Mickiewicz (1798--1855), whom he met and played for in Paris around 1835. The English composer and author Alan Rawsthorne noted, however, that \\"to pin down these Ballades to definite stories is gratuitous and misleading, fur in suggesting extra-musical connotations the attention is distracted from the purely musical scheme which is ... compelling in itself and completely satisfying?' Rather than obscuring the essential nature of these pieces, the apparently opposing views of Schumann and Rawsthorne lead directly to the very heart of Chopin's achievement: the near-perfect melding of Romantic fantasy and feeling with an Apollonian control of form and figuration. By no other composer in the history of the art has the delicate balance between emotion and intellect been so finely achieved as by Chopin -- heart and head are weighed perfectly in his, the most precisely calibrated of all musical scales.<br /><br />The first ideas for Ballade No. 1 in C minor, Opus 23 were sketched in May and June 1831, when Chopin was living anxiously in Vienna, almost unknown as a composer and only slightly appreciated as a pianist. By the time that the work was completed four years later, however, he had achieved such fame and fortune in Paris that he could dedicate the piece to Baron de Stockhausen, the Hanoverian ambassador to France, whom he counted among his noble pupils. Breitkopf und Hartel published the work in Leipzig in June 1836. (Chalgrin's Arc de Triomphe and Meyerheer's Les Huguenots were also completed during that year).<br /><br />Schumann called this Ballade \\"the most spirited and daring work of Chopin,\\" and reported that it was inspired by Mickiewicz's Kon rad Velenrod, a poetic epic concerning the battles between the pagan Lithuanians and the Christian Knights of the Teutonic Order. The work exhibits both the ingenious conflation of sectional, sonata, and rondo forms and the voluptuous, wide-ranging harmonic palette that mark all of the Ballades.<br /><br />This recording is a masterpiece.ayed for in Paris around 1835. The English composer and author Alan Rawsthorne noted, however, that \\"to pin down these Ballades to definite stories is gratuitous and misleading, fur in suggesting extra-musical connotations the attention is distracted from the purely musical scheme which is ... compelling in itself and completely satisfying?' Rather than obscuring the essential nature of these pieces, the apparently opposing views of Schumann and Rawsthorne lead directly to the very heart of Chopin's achievement: the near-perfect melding of Romantic fantasy and feeling with an Apollonian control of form and figuration. By no other composer in the history of the art has the delicate balance between emotion and intellect been so finely achieved as by Chopin -- heart and head are weighed perfectly in his, the most precisely calibrated of all musical scales. This recording is a masterpiece.

Krist Novoselic's bass sounds amazing on \\"I Should Have Known\\", that just blew me away.<br /><br />It's a 2 LP set so you'll be flipping records every couple songs, and even though it's a 12'' the speed setting is 45 RPM's. I knew that going into it but I still forget to switch my turntable from 33 so it sounded really slooooow. I haven't listened to another record since I got this one a couple days ago but I know I'm going to end up forgetting to switch it back to 33 when I'm done with it.<br /><br />The packing is great. Each record is in it's own record white sleeve but then there is also different sleeves that include Foo Fighters artwork on them as well, but the records don't come shipped in those sleeves, which is how it should be. There is also a paper to download a free copy of the album in 320k mp3 format which is actually ripped from the vinyl which is pretty cool.<br /><br />The band did a great job with this release. A really great vinyl and the tunes are top notch. Well worth the $20.

145162445X|2.0|However correct the cosmological quantum or particle physics being developed these days might be this author has no right to redefine a commonly understood word to serve his purpose of sensationalizing his book as he so presumptuously does in its title \\"Why There Is Something Rather Than Nothing\\". One can write a lot of remarkable sounding stuff if one is allowed to redefine simple concepts.   Nothing is just the contraction of the idea \\"no thing\\".  It's not the \\"absence of something\\" as he writes but the absence of all things. It is not a theological concept (he likes to write stuff like \\"theologians idealized nothing\\" since he knows that the term \\"theologian\\" already tends to discredit) or  some metaphysical subtlety like \\"substance\\", or \\"transubstantiation\\", and not \\"empty space\\", and nothing like the more complex if vague notion of intelligent design - Krauss likes to mix in a lot of stuff for confusion - but the simple abstract concept all humans have naturally. The author's redefined \\"nothing\\" is a kind of zero information soup of particles coming in and out of existence, which he should honestly create his own term for, an not usurp a well understood term to sound more sensational.   He writes in his remarkably sophomoric preface stuff like 'For surely \\"nothing\\" is every bit as physical as \\"something\\", especially if it is to be defined as the \\"absence of something\\" '.  I suspect this is a sign of mental disease.   Actually, he  admits the simple concept of nothing when he writes \\"So be it\\" (pg. xiv) . He  may be just dishonestly over-hyping and not insane, but I'm not sure.  He enjoys dismissing the First Cause argument as begging the question and delights in referring to the \\"turtles all the way down\\" counter point. Of course the First Cause has been asserted to contain, as it must, its own necessity for existence - something nothing else in the universe clearly has. And yes since we cannot even imagine how a being can contain its own necessity to exist, this First Cause is not a scientific concept or hypothesis.  I'm not a religious person so I'm fine with a person's desire to reflect only on scientific concepts, but such a person doesn't really have a way I see given in this book to know why the laws of physics are what they are. What such people like the author seems to me to be doing is first ASSUMING there is no First Cause creator, and then pushing that, with some quantum science plus speculations, to conclusions like \\"Perhaps the laws arouse themselves spontaneously\\". But this is really a sort of theology itself.

B005HV6Y5W|5.0|You can see from the bar graph showing the star ratings of the reviews here that this film elicits strong feelings, both positive and negative.  Although the film's overall rating is 3 stars as of this writing (January 2012), the 3-star rating itself has the smallest number of reviews, with the 5-star and 1-star reviews about equally divided, as are the 4-star and 2-star reviews.  It's not that most people found the film to be average, when in fact very few people did.  They either loved it or hated it.    It's unusual for a film to elicit such a polarized response, but it's not surprising that this film would.  Most people seem to agree that the film is filled with beautiful images (and they are stunning), but many people understandably expect more from a movie than gorgeous cinematography.  It appears to me that there are three main sections to the film:  a fairly long introduction, very visual and with only short sequences involving the characters, minimal dialogue and some brief and somewhat enigmatic voice-overs;  a longer middle section, in which the actors are seen in a series of separate but related episodes arranged in more or less chronological order;  and a conclusion, which returns to the highly visual style of the introduction, with the addition of more scenes including the characters, but again, very little dialogue, just voice-overs.    There is a story, but it's not shown in a tightly connected, linear manner.  The director assumes that viewers will fill in the blanks between the scenes, and he provides enough of a structure that this is not something that requires a great deal of speculation by the viewer.  Still, I think this section is the one that viewers may find most challenging (or annoying, depending on one's frame of mind), and I can see that it's not something that would appeal to everyone.  The first time I watched the film, I stopped after about 15 minutes, because I was tired, I had trouble paying attention, and I really wasn't sure it was going to be worth my time or my effort.  I'm very glad that I didn't give up on it, because the second time I watched it, I couldn't stop watching.    The acting is consistently excellent, and I found myself forgetting that it was Brad Pitt, because his portrayal of a 1950's father was so convincing.  The same was true of the mother and the actors who played the children.  It felt at times as if I were viewing a very beautifully presented documentary with high production values.    The soundtrack is a major part of the film, with passages from Mahler, Brahms, and others, and with such a soundtrack, and so many beautiful visual sequences, it's easy to see why this film had been compared to \\"2001.\\"  This is not a science fiction film, and it does not deal with space travel, but both films share a similar style.    The director took on a major project here, he did not flinch from challenging viewers, and ultimately, I believe that he was successful. It's a film I will definitely watch more than one time.   The Blu-Ray disc is outstanding, with excellent image quality and audio.  My only complaint is that there is only one 30-minute feature, and I think that a film like this would have an ideal candidate for a more comprehensive set of extra features.

0385344228|5.0|\\"Defending Jacob\\" is one of those rare novels that grabs your attention from the beginning, is almost impossible to put down, and then haunts you long after you've finished it.<br /><br />Author William Landay opens with an enigma: a former assistant district attorney is being grilled by an old colleague in front of a grand jury.  The ex-A.D.A. tells us that his testimony is too late; he then begins to narrate a convoluted tale revolving around the murder of a teen-aged boy ... a crime that his own son is eventually accused of committing.  We then flash back to the time of the murder, and the father walks us through the investigation and the trial and its aftermath, along a path riddled with twists and turns and surprises - including some shocking truths that are rooted deep in the past.  The genius of the plot is that Landay keeps referring the reader back to the book's opening pages, keeping us constantly off guard by reminding us that we're being teased along by a mystery that is embedded in a larger puzzle.  The final revelations are deliciously and thrillingly unexpected.<br /><br />\\"Defending Jacob\\" is a superb blend of mystery and suspense mixed with enough courtroom drama and family psychology to satisfy devotees of all these genres.  I heartily recommend it, without reservation.  It deserves a wide audience, as well as all the awards that are almost certainly destined to be bestowed upon its author.

This is a book that, without giving spoilers, explores some of the deepest and most asked questions of human existence while never being condescending or self-righteous, and with no attempts to merely hand the reader the answers. TFiOS is that rarest of books in which you can both laugh and cry on the same page, a book that draws the reader so intimately in that in the course of the novel it becomes real. John Green's works exhibit an uncanny knack for making the fictional world become real for the reader, and TFiOS is no exception. It develops complex and believable characters with assets and flaws the same as any real person. TFiOS is truly exceptional in the sense that it understands that \\"Young Adult\\" does not mean that the reader is uniformed or ill-equipped to handle real themes and real issues. Much like Paper Towns and Looking For Alaska, TFiOS gives the reader just enough discussion of deep and meaningful questions to encourage exploration while offering new insight into the world in which we live. John Green's ability to imagine complex characters is on par with the greatest of all literature. TFiOS is, in short, a masterpiece: beautifully sculpted as only a genius could create.

B0068RHGJM|4.0|In terms of musical growth, Imaginerium is not really far off from Dark Passion Play.  The musical style is just what you would normally expect from Nightwish.  This is not necessarily a bad thing; every album I've ever heard from Nightwish feels like it has taken an immense amount of work.  Nothing is ever done half-heartedly.  While, I am more of an \\"old Nightwish\\" fan, I can still very much enjoy their new work.  Since a lot of what I was thinking about this album has already been said by other reviewers, I'll just go through the major strengths and weaknesses instead of through a list of all the songs.  Strengths:  - The songs on this album have actually been written for Anette's vocal range so it no longer sounds as though she is straining herself all the time (as it did in Dark Passion Play).  While I wouldn't go as far as to say I really like her singing much, she does sound much more comfortable and there are a few places that she does shine - mostly on the slow songs when she is not competing with the orchestra.  \\"The Crow, The Owl, and The Dove\\" is a beautiful piece incorporating the best Anette and Marco's singing.  Marco, as usual, is a wonderful vocalist and I'm glad they gave him the amount of singing he deserved.  - Yeah, yeah slow songs.  We know we all want the epic, highly orchestrated pieces that Nightwish is well known for.  \\"Ghost River\\" is one of my favorite songs on the album.  Marco sounds deliciously evil with his vocals and the layered complexity of the song is perfectly done.  It does take a few listens - it did not immediately stand out to me as the best song on the album.  \\"I Want My Tears Back\\" is another highly catchy, addictive song with celtic motifs very similar to \\"Last of the Wilds\\" from DPP.  I disagree with some of the other reviewers who didn't like the celtic feel - I think Nightwish pulls it off very well and I would like to see more of it in the future.  \\"Last Ride of the Day\\" is another very typical, catchy and epic Nightwish song.  I also liked \\"Rest Calm\\", despite its weird mix of a metal verse with a slow, gentle chorus (the chorus is very memorable).  - We have yet to see how these tracks will be incorporated into the movie but from what I've heard, I think this album will be awesome as a sound track.  The flow between songs is well done.  The creepy carnival feel occurs several times.  There are multiple tracks with snatches of a children's chorus.  There are songs you feel really would create a certain mood in a movie - \\"Scaretale\\" and \\"Arabesque\\" being two.  Weaknesses:  - I think the major flaw of this album is the lack of that one big signature epic Nightwish song that ties everything together.  Century Child had \\"Beauty of the Beast\\", Once had \\"Ghost Love Score\\", and Dark Passion Play had \\"The Poet and the Pendulum.\\"  Tuomas certainly does try with \\"Song of Myself\\", which clocks in at 13 minutes.  A good 7 of those minutes are just poetic talking that gets very boring after you've heard it once.  The song itself falls rather flat.  It's loud and bangy and big but it doesn't really have a memorable melody or much interesting about it at all.  Also, the first single of the album - \\"Storytime\\" is a good song but it just really isn't up to the standard set by \\"Amaranth\\" in DPP.  - I know I put Anette's singing as improved from DPP on my list of strengths.  So that strength was somewhat relative.  I still feel like Anette's singing is a weakness of the band.  With minimal orchestration she can do well but that's not what Nightwish is about.  In the highly orchestrated, big numbers she seems to be fighting a losing battle to be heard.  And I know her weird scratchy singing in \\"Scaretale\\" was there to match the feel of the song but it still makes me cringe.  It isn't pleasant to listen to at all.  - There are two many slow songs on this album.  I know that we do need to stop for breath sometime but still.  \\"Slow Love Slow\\" is just plain boring, as everyone has said, despite havingan experimental bluesy feel for Nightwish.  I just skip straight through it.  \\"Turn loose the mermaids\\", while more musically interesting that \\"Slow Love Slow\\" still seems a bit tedious.  I did like \\"Taikatalvi\\" (good intro and always like Nightwish giving a tip of the hat to their Finnish origin) and \\"The Crow, the Owl, and the Dove\\" - with the usual Marco trademark of far better lyrics than most Tuomas songs.  Come to think of it, Tuomas should really let Marco do all the slow song writing and maybe have a greater hand in lyrics overall.  Although I really like the melody of \\"I Want My Tears Back\\", the way those lyrics fit into the song is particularly cringe-worthy.  Bottom line: I expect this will be a must-have for all Nightwish fans and I really look forward to seeing how the tracks fits into the movie.  It is a solid album, though not Nightwish's best.  For those who have never heard Nightwish before, my favorites are Once, Dark Passion Play, and Century Child, in that order.

B0058WFO06|5.0|This is certainly one of those albums you might not \\"get\\" the first couple listens, even if you're already a fan. I was a bit worried about this one, to be honest. The last solo album (Insurgentes) contained a few amazing tracks, but I routinely skip half of them. When I heard Steven Wilson was doing a bunch of King Crimson work I cringed and said to myself \\"Oh, well that explains it.\\" I never really enjoyed KC all that much, and the knowledge that this album would probably contain quite a lot of influence from them stained my initial reception.  Perhaps I'm getting older. Maybe now I would like KC better than I remember, but for whatever reason, I really love Grace for Drowning. I find myself waking up with \\"Sectarian\\" already playing in my head. There is a sax (or maybe clarinet) solo that gets in my head and won't go away. \\"Deform to Form a Star\\" has some very beautiful harmony vocals (all Wilson, I believe) with a gorgeous electric piano bit at the 4:30 point that I am going to have to learn how to play. \\"Belle de Jour\\" is a phenomenally beautiful piece, somehow sad and uplifting at the same time.  At one point I would have said there isn't a stand-out track that I can think of. I really quite like them all, individually rating the songs between 4 and 5 stars in iTunes. But the more I listen, the more one track stands out above the rest: Raider II. It's odd, dark, foreboding, highly experimental, eclectic, and many other things. But this is certainly a track that one must study numerous times before making a judgement. It builds very, very slowly and ominously into something amazing and unique that I quite frankly don't have the words to describe. This song goes just about everywhere during its 23 minutes of life. It will challenge you and take you to musical places you may have never gone before. Most amazing is that this song has become the most requested by my children (10 and 7 years old): they love it, and I hear them humming the primary theme several timesduring the course of a day. If there's nothing else to be satisfied with, it's the fact that my kids are choosing music like this over the substance-free crap their peers all listen to.  Grace for Drowning will challenge you and take you to musical places you may never have been before. For this reason, I almost guarantee you probably won't get it the few listens. To be certain, I wasn't even sure I LIKED it the first few listens. It's not easy to listen to, by any means, and if you're not in the mood to be challenged, save it for a time when you are more prepared. After a dozen or so listens, I'm prepared to say that this is most certainly a masterpiece at all levels.

B0068RGVMK|5.0|First of all, let me say that this is a huge improvement from \\"Dark Passion Play.\\" I'll admit, while Anette does come across as a sweet, likable, vivacious woman, her contemporary vocal style is just not my cup of tea. I'm a much bigger fan of Tarja's unique, majestic operatic vocals, but this album does give me more respect for Anette.<br /><br />Of course, the heart and soul of Nightwish is the leader, keyboardist, and main songwriter of the band, Tuomas Holopainen. Not only are his compositions magically cinematic, his lyrics tell pages after pages of wondrous stories, often with themes that focus on childhood and dreams and nightmares. This theme continues to Nightwish's seventh studio album, Imaginaerum. It is a concept album that tells the story of a composer who falls victim to old age and severe dementia. As he is dying, he loses all of his memory of his adult life, his family, his friends, and his daughter, until he has nothing left but the memories and imagination of his ten-year-old self.<br /><br />Imaginaerum is not only an album; it is also an upcoming film of the same name and same story, with music also provided by Nightwish and their newest album. However, while the album accompanies the film, it can also be enjoyed on its own. Here is a review of the thirteen tracks from, not just a beautiful album, but also from a touching story that I hope won't disappoint when it hits the big screens. I did some lyrical analysis, much of which I could have misinterpreted, but this album definitely speaks to me, as I have a fondness for childhood nostalgia and imagination.<br /><br />1. Taikatalvi<br />The opening track, which means \\"Winter Magic\\" in Finnish, begins with the windings of a music box. As a lullaby-like melody tinkles out, Marco Hietala, the male vocalist of Nightwish, demonstrates his capability to sing gentler songs. Although the entire song is in Finnish, the song is so reminiscent of a magical winter night that, despite language barriers, one could feel as though one is being swept up by the enchanted snowflakes of a winter wonderland.<br />5/5<br /><br />2. Storytime<br />The lullaby from the last track leaps into an energetic, optimistic song of childhood imagination. This is the leading single from the album, sung by Anette Olzon, the female vocalist of the band. The lyrics references fictional characters such as Peter Pan, Alice from Alice in Wonderland, and Gaia from Greek mythology. Despite its contagious bubbliness, and perhaps being one of the most radio-friendly tracks on the album, the entire song is merely the opening act to a circus of dreams and wonders.<br />4/5<br /><br />3. Ghost River<br />Power metal kicks in with guitars at the beginning. A duet between Anette Olzon and Marco Hietala demonstrates a popular motif in the gothic metal genre known as \\"Beauty and the Beast vocals,\\" which is used to describe a contrast between angelic female vocals and agressive, growling male vocals. Unsurprisingly, this song has been described as \\"a duel between the Devil and Mother Gaia.\\" Later on, they are joined by a children's choir to add a layer of innocence to this theatrical number.<br />5/5<br /><br />4. Slow, Love, Slow<br />This is Nightwish's first jazz-influenced song, inspired by 1930's American nightclub music. While many Nightwish fans debate that Anette's contemporary vocal style is unsuitable for the metal genre, there is no question that Anette is in her element here. Smooth, smoldering, and seductive, she is elegantly accompanied by a sultry backdrop painted by the piano and the saxophone.<br />3/5<br /><br />5. I Want My Tears Back<br />Personally, I felt like this is a cringe-worthy track. While I loved the Celtic instrumental interlude, this whole song just sounded too bubbly, way more bubbly than \\"Storytime.\\" And I cringe a lot at this Alice in Wonderland reference: \\"Where is the wonder where`s the awe/Where`s dear Alice knocking on the door/ Where`s the trapdoor that takes me there/Where the real is shattered by a Mad March Hare.\\"<br />2/5<br /><br />6. Scaretale<br />A dark, bombastic seven-and-a-half minute song about childhood nightmares. It starts with a children's choir, singing \\"Ring Around the Rosy\\" as if they were broken mechanical dolls, and then vocalizing as they drag the listener deeper into a moody, circus-y, Danny Elfman-esque atmosphere. Anette sounds particular witchy, and sings of a children-eating bride, squealing pigs, a pool of snakes, and fallen mirrors. Welcome to Cirque De Morgue!<br />5/5<br /><br />7. Arabesque<br />The macabre topsy-turvy traverses into a dramatic Middle-Eastern-influenced instrumental track. It sounds as if a frenzied chase is ensuing across miles of desert sands underneath foreboding moonlight, all just underneath 3 minutes.<br />5/5<br /><br />8. Turn Loose the Mermaids<br />The previous turbulence mellows out into a sweet ballad of a kite flying high in the sky, a long journey, and a life long-lived. Though this is a mere conjecture, perhaps the line \\"The mermaids you turned loose brought back your tears\\" is meant to metaphorically describe nostalgia and the recollection of long-lost memories, which is perhaps what this song is meant to induce in the listener.<br />5/5<br /><br />9. Rest Calm<br />At this point, the elderly songwriter, who is the central character of this concept album, is upon his deathbed. He reminisces of his memories, which he hopes will never let him go. At the climax of the song, the children choir sound like the manifestation of nostalgia, making the old man's memories rest in his dreams and smile back at him as if nothing else exists.<br />5/5<br /><br />10. The Crow, the Owl, and the Dove<br />A breathtakingly beautiful ballad between Marco and Anette. It opens with an acoustic guitar, and both of them bittersweetly ask not for love, faith, wisdom, pride, beauty, nor rest, but for innocence and truth. They encounter a crow,  an owl, and a dove, respectively envying its pride, sense, and love, but realizing they needed nothing they had. At the climax of the song, guest vocalist Troy Donockley sings in another language, \\"Go to the river, you'll find fairyland.\\"<br />5/5<br /><br />11. Last Ride of the Day<br />Inspired by being on a roller coaster, this song was written to describe approaching the end of life and enjoying all that life has to offer. Existence is described as a beautiful theme park ride with an \\"incredible high,\\" and the \\"carnival of life\\" consists of us chasing our dreams, in an adventureland of metaphorical tricksters, magicians, jugglers, and snakecharmers.<br />5/5<br /><br />12. Song of Myself<br />This is the final song that features vocals, and is a 14-minute epic divided into four parts, each respectively titled 1) From a Dusty Bookshelf, 2) All That Great Heart Still Lying, 3) Piano Black, and 4) Love. Remnants of a great imagination, and a piano slowly rusting after the death of the its master, is lyrically showcased here. Songwriter Tuomas Holopainen says that this was inspired by the Walt Whitman song of the same name, and after Anette's voice bows off the stage, segments of the poem is recited as an orchestra solemnly plays. I kind of wish the poem recitation wasn't included. Without it, the song would have just concluded neatly and elegantly, but it just drags and becomes overkill. I wish I could give it 5 stars, but...<br />4/5<br /><br />13. Imaginaerum<br />The final track is an instrumental medley of the Imaginaerum`s main musical themes. Each of the previous tracks is musically referenced here, like a waltz through the flipping pages of a richly illustrated storybook. This is definitely a ride upon a enchanted roller coaster through a theme park of life, rich with a child's imagination and a heart that never ceases to love and create and dream. This is a circus of wonders, an uplifting ride upon angelic wings that circles around multi-hued castles, soars across an ocean, and gazes out into a transient, brilliant sunset that blazes just as it immerses into an evening ocean of moonlight and a starry sky. What a beautiful way to end an album.<br />5/5<br /><br />I only hope that the film will live up to the magical adventure!<br /><br />Here is a modified review that I wrote for UC Berkeley's Caliber Magazine: [...]r, you'll find fairyland.\\"  5/5     11. B0000CABBW|5.0|I've shown this movie to one or more of my classes probably a dozen times and it's still enjoyable.<br /><br />The story is set among a group of Maori in present-day New Zealand. The two main characters are the chief of this group (\\"Paka\\") and his troublesome 13-year old granddaughter (\\"Pai\\"). The grandfather loves Pai as a granddaughter but also blames her for \\"the troubles,\\" which he believes started the night she was born. (Her brother, who should have become the heir, died at birth, as did the mother; the father, Paka's eldest son, was so distraught that he fled New Zealand and established himself as an up-and-coming artist in Europe -- abandoning his people, in Paka's view.)<br /><br />From the beginning, Pai shows unusual talents and interest in \\"the old ways,\\" but Paka cannot see it because of his obsession with a male heir. At one point he gives up on his family and starts a school to find the potential heir among the first-born sons of the tribe -- with rather unpredictable results. If Amazon had a 6 star rating, I'd give this movie 6.<br /><br />The casting of this movie is brilliant. The conflict between Paka and Pai sizzles and the supporting characters (notably Paka's wife and Paka's two sons, the father and the uncle) are brilliant. Even the walk-on characters, like the other tribe members and the boys who attend the school are great.<br /><br />The scenery is fabulous and the story unfolds in a beautiful way. The movie is enjoyable on so many levels -- as good entertainment, as an inspiring story, as an interesting perspective on a different culture, or as a story on the generational conflict, families, and love.

B001FB55H6|3.0|Making a film of this graphics novel is like trying to condense Proust's Remembrance of Things Past into a Classics Illustrated comic book.  The graphic novel is so well constructed, so packed with story, so multi-layered, and so well put together, that it would really take about a 7 hour movie to do justice to it.  In order to fit the Director's Cut into roughly 3 hours, all of the Tales of the Black Freighter subplot has been removed as well as all the inter-chapter materials from the book, which provides an incredible amount of important back story.<br /><br />That obviously creates a problem, because the counter story of Tales of the Black Freighter are incredibly important elements of the whole plot. You think I'm locked up in here with you. I've not seen that, but I think it would improve the experience of the story a lot.<br /><br />But the very best thing to do would be to read the graphic novel before watching any version of the movie. 0312641893|5.0|Despite being based on one of the most famous romantic fairy tales of all time, Cinder by Marissa Meyer proves to be a fresh and exciting read for readers of all sorts. I love to read, but I'm a slow reader and a reader that needs real excitement in order to finish a book. That said, I'm happy to report that I finished this book in two days. This means, I REALLY liked it.<br /><br />Cinder starts off quickly and the easy, swift pacing is held throughout the book. I'm also not usually a fan of sci-fi stories, finding them hard to relate to and get lost in, but Meyer does a fantastic job of creating a vivid reality of Cinder's world while still keeping the book easy to follow and absorb. What's more, the author seems to make Cinder's world so real by creating unique technology that is still easy for us to relate to. For example, in Cinder's world they rely on \\"portscreens\\" and \\"netscreens\\" for communication, news, etc. I found it realistic and easy to imagine these things as future advancements on tablet computers and our modern flatscreen TVs and computers. It's very easy to see Cinder's life in New Beijing as a very realistic possibility for earth's future. This makes the sci-fi aspect of the book easier to handle for folks like me, who usually are lost in sci-fi fantasy.<br /><br />Cinder's story is very much like the classic Cinderella in many ways, she has an evil stepmother who keeps her as a slave, two stepsisters, and an uncertain past. When her paths cross with Prince Kai, Cinder is thrust into an unlikely romance. I have read a few reviews here that criticize the ease and \\"love at first sight\\" quality to Kai's attraction to Cinder. To this I have to say, come on, this is the retelling of a fairy tale! It's supposed to be \\"love at first sight\\" and \\"true love\\", and the reader should be expecting this. Much to my pleasant surprise, while Kai is immediately smitten with Cinder, she--on the other hand--does NOT turn into some swoony, pathetic girl in love with the handsome prince (I'm talking to you, Bella Swan). Thank you, Marissa Meyer! Too many heroines of the popular young adult novels feature whiny, weak girls for young readers to look up to. While Kai is the charming, strong hero I look for in a romantic book, Cinder is refreshingly intellectual and thoughtful.<br /><br />I think many teen readers will enjoy and relate to the character of Cinder because, despite being cyborg (human with artificial body parts), she is a very real character to read. She is teased and mocked by her enemies because of her artificial body parts, and seeing how so many teens (and adults, too) struggle with self-image, I think Cinder is a heroine MANY will so easily relate to. She struggles to love herself at times, because of her cyborg parts and her downfalls, but when it really counts Cinder stands up for herself. I love this!<br /><br />The pacing throughout all 38 chapters is swift and kept me unable to put my Kindle down until I finished this story, so readers in need of excitement and romance will definitely be happy with this read. Cinder's story is full of a few surprises and twists, though it also has a few non-surprises, at the end we are left with a cliffhanger. This is one difference from the classic Cinderella story. Cinder's first book does not leave us with a \\"happily ever after\\" complete with a bow. Instead, we are still hopeful for that \\"happily ever after\\", but it's obvious there is more in store for Cinder and Kai before that is even possible. This was fine by me, however, seeing as I rushed through the book so quickly, I'm excited to read more of what happens to Cinder and Kai and I'm hoping Meyer can deliver as well as she did with this first book!<br /><br />Finally, as a mother of two young daughters, I'm very happy to say that this book gets the mom seal of approval from me! Cinder is not without it's dark and intense moments, but Meyer respectfully wrote a clean (I'm talking language and intimacy) book that I woulddefinitely share with a reader even as young as 10. Too many young adult books have been showing up on shelves lately, full of cursing and promiscuity. It's nice to see an author finally show her talent without trying to sellout to what the publishing world seems to THINK teens want/need in a book. Which reminds me, the writing was clear, concise, and impressive. I would compare it to the style of Suzanne Collins of the famous Hunger Games books. Meyer clearly has talent.<br /><br />I would absolutely recommend this fresh new novel to the young readers, AND adult readers who are interested in any mixture of adventure, fairy tales, sci-fi, and romanticism. I think it's a fun, fascinating read, and it kept me on edge for most of the book. I'm very much looking forward to the release of Meyer's next book--let's hope it doesn't take too long!s 10. Which reminds me, the writing was clear, concise, and impressive. I would compare it to the style of Suzanne Collins of the famous Hunger Games books. Meyer clearly has talent.   I would absolutely recommend this fresh new novel to the young readers, AND adult readers who are interested in any mixture of adventure, fairy tales, sci-fi, and romanticism. I think it's a fun, fascinating read, and it kept me on edge for most of the book. I'm very much looking forward to the release of Meyer's next book--let's hope it doesn't take too long!

1451648537|3.0|Steve Jobs asked Walter Isaacson to research and write his biography before the Apple CEO died, but it's doubtful that Jobs would be pleased with the result. I'm not sure that Isaacson is either, as the book was obviously rushed to press with poor editing. Isaacson had more than 50 interviews with Jobs, who also told everyone he had ever known that they should speak to the author. So \\"Steve Jobs\\", the book, benefits from unparalleled access. Isaacson presents Jobs as \\"a creative entrepreneur whose passion for perfection and ferocious drive revolutionized six industries: personal computers, animated movies, music, phones, tablet computing, and digital publishing\\", and as a terrible person, which is one of the book's weaknesses.<br /><br />Beginning with the odd circumstances of his adoption by the Jobs family of Mountain View, California and continuing through Steve Jobs' counterculture youth until his collaboration with Steve Wozniak on the first Apple computer in 1975, we get a picture of a bright, obsessive young man who finds it easy to impose his will on the people and places around him. It seems just as well, as no one could ever impose his will on Steve. Isaacson focuses primarily on Jobs' professional trials and accomplishments, with side trips into his personal life, as he and Wozniak found Apple Computer, create the first fully packaged computer in the Apple II, Jobs develops the Macintosh, a blow-by-blow account of his ouster from Apple, at NeXT, Pixar, and Jobs return to Apple in 1997.<br /><br />All of the major product developments from that point on are discussed: the iMac, Apple's retail stores, the iPod, iTunes, iPhone, and iPad. At some point, the book stops being strictly chronological, as it addresses each development by topic. In addition to great access, I like Isaacson's emphasis on design. Other books about Steve Jobs have disappointingly short-changed the subject, when design was clearly foremost in Jobs' mind. I wanted to understand his philosophy of design, and this book goes into depth about the development of the Macintosh and Jobs' collaboration with designer Jony Ive from the iMac forward. Isaacson faithfully reflects Jobs' obsession on this point, which I enjoyed.<br /><br />The book's shortcomings are not insignificant, however. Isaacson does not come from a business or technology background, so he does not say as much as he could about Apple's impact on the industries it touched. And Walter Isaacson does not like Steve Jobs. Yes, Jobs was insufferable, but Isaacson seems more put off by his nastiness than the people whom Jobs mistreated. The book is longer than it needs to be. There is a lot of superfluous verbiage, redundancy, imprecision. One chapter, oddly, just showcases Jobs' musical tastes and his relationships with recording artists. Impressive access. I love the emphasis on design. But not well-written, sloppily edited, and too chiding at times. \\"Steve Jobs\\" is worthwhile for Apple fans and design fanatics, though.osophy of design, and this book goes into depth about the development of the Macintosh and Jobs' collaboration with designer Jony Ive from the iMac forward. Our galaxy is one of about 400 billion. Hubble's Law (1929) found a linear relationship between recessional velocity and galaxy distance - distant galaxies are moving away at faster velocities. When a star explodes, over the course of a month or so it shines with a brightness of 10 billion stars, and is the brightest phenomena in the universe, is referred to as a 'standard star,'and used to estimate the speed of recession. An exploding star occurs about once every 100 years within each galaxy. Only the lightest elements (hydrogen, deuterium - heavy hydrogen, helium, and lithium) were made in the Big Bang - the rest were formed via fusion in the stars. About 200 million stars have exploded to date.<br /><br />A circle about the size of a dime made at arm's length and examined by one of today's larger telescopes would contain about 100,000 galaxies; the viewer would see about 3 exploding stars/night. (I think his talk on U-tube stated '10.') Astronomers estimate that galaxies are moving at about 70 km/second - about 151,000 mph. Within about 2 trillion years these accelerating galaxies will reach the speed of light and no longer be visible. The fact that they're accelerating proves that 'empty space' contains vast amounts of dark energy.<br /><br />Starlight was observed to curve around the Sun during a 1919 solar eclipse, by exactly the amount predicted by Einstein in his theory of general relativity.<br /><br />The universe could be of three types - closed (would allow a person who looked far enough in one direction to see the back of his head), flat, or open. What ultimately makes it work is the director's steady hand, his willingness and courage to test the audience's patience as he slowly builds up the plot, just as George Smiley patiently built his strategy to track down the Russian mole.

B0060ZJ74O|4.0|'Moneyball' underwent as many benching's and roster changes in the production stage as the Oakland A's did in their 2002 pursuit of a World Series as depicted in the film. In 2011, finally completed, `Moneyball' is a baseball movie that transcends just professional sports, but becomes a simile for determination, integrity, and life in general. This is a sports movie that elicits inspiration, not in the typical Rocky banality, but with reflective affinity that resonates with us all.  The film, based on true events, follows the small market Oakland Athletics' General Manager Billy Beane in his pursuit of a World Series with the lowest budget of any team in the league. When he is forced to gut his team after a successful season, he does so by hiring a new assistant to help him develop an entirely revolutionary concept of recruiting players based on number crunching methods. This new method derides classic scouting methods that were based on intuition and professional experience.  The film is unorthodox in that it uses the game of baseball and its players as a backdrop to what the general managers and scouts do behind the dugout in their little offices. While this film focuses on the cutthroat struggle of team management, its strength lies in the pitch perfect (pun intended) execution of the screenplay which resembles closely what 'The Social Network' did for all of its intricate computer vernacular and idioms. It is no coincidence that Aaron Sorkin was credited as a writer, making accessible all the dialogue involving player statistics and complex business transactions. This is a drama through and through, but the pacing is supplemented with understated humor, a subtle buddy complex between Jonah Hill and Brad Pitt, and some excellent shots of actual baseball (consisting of real game footage, and recreated scenes, equipped with slow motion and portrait like opaque backgrounds for epicness).  Inspiration is a key theme in most sports films because it is fundamentally inherent in the sporting world in general. Unfortunately for a lot idealistic films about (_insert sport here_), they tend to resort to cliché's about larger than life golden boys who defeat insurmountable odds to the delight of everyone around them. `Moneyball' succeeds in lifting the spirits by defying this rule. Billy Beane is, of course, painted in a way that's easy to root for, he's the ultimate underdog - but he pisses a lot of people off along the way. He is human to us because he makes decisions that have consequences; this is a film that isn't afraid to see the protagonists' worst case scenario materialized. This film unearths greatness due to excellent performances, succinct execution, and an exceptionally grounded story.   If I may resort to clichés myself, Bennett Miller's `Moneyball' is a definite winner. No...it's a home run!

There are some some lovely moments, such as a mini-story about the town of cats, but so much fluff to wade through with typical Murakami characters that are just full of longing. The two main characters pine for each other after a brief encounter when they were 10. Highly self-conscious writing, such as when characters talk about how a gun is used after it's introduced because of dear Chekov. Worst of all, only tangentially related to Orwell's 1984.  Book 3 was even more disappointing. The book now adds another viewpoint; a suspicious character who we are constantly reminded looks ugly and it not accepted in Japanese society. Also, the plot slows to a crawl, as both characters seem content to hideout. Plot threads are left dangling, and the prime focus is the supposed relationship between the two main characters, who held each other's hand briefly when they were 10.  I really wanted to like this book. Anybody who devotes their time to read such a huge tome would. In fact, there were interesting components; a doomsday cult led by a charismatic leader, a mysterious alternate Japan, shady gangster types, a matriarch determined to take revenge for the cause of abused women but Murakami drifts off to focus on the thin love affair between the two leads. There are wonderful moments, such as the story about a town of cats, or Tengo's Sundays wandering with his father to collect television subscription money, and bits of wonderful description, but those are few and far between. Instead what we get is some fairy tale about little people, and characters that are two-dimensional and quite unconvincing. This book was a real struggle to get through. It seemed to be more of an experiment in literary devices than something that was meant to be enjoyed. The idea of six linked stories covering different eras sounds really cool, but the actual content was pretty boring.   The first two stories are kind of slow, not very interesting. The third and fourth are a little more promising in the first half of the book, but by the end are too drawn-out to be engaging. The fifth and sixth are truly awful. The fifth story (the dystopian one) is really derivative of  pretty much everything else I've read in the genre, but doesn't add anything new or unique to the genre. It's just a weak imitation. The sixth one (set in Hawaii) is written to convey a speech pattern and was nearly impossible for me to read.   In the end, I found all the stories to be nothing more than tedious copies of existing stuff. Mitchell didn't add anything new, just jumbled a bunch of conventions together. The linking of the stories really was nothing more than little mentions here and there, nothing profound. The whole book added up to something extraordinarily pretentious with very little to actually say (certainly nothing original). Then Mitchell used a bunch of 'clever' literary tricks piled on top in an attempt to disguise this. If it's supposed to play with genres, I found it very conventional and really lacking in any joy or fun. Maybe it's more enjoyable if you really, really like literary devices.    This was a serious struggle to get through, and at the end all I felt was relief! I'm really baffled by all the great reviews - I thought this was pretty terrible and disappointing. 2/5
