0470424656|5.0|This book and Damodaran's Investment Valuation are the main valuation texts used in many of the top MBA programs.  Of the two, this is my favorite.  Part 2 of the book, Core Valuation Techniques, is particularly good.  The authors discuss the framework for valuation, how to assess historical performance, and how to forecast financial statements.  The comprehensive example discussed throughout these chapters shows readers how to handle the nuances of many non-operating components of a business such as the pension liability, excess cash, discontinued operations, etc.  The final chapter in the section proves that multiples, when used correctly, require the same inputs as a DCF model eliminating the critique that DCF valuations are untenable due to their heroic assumptions.    The second half of the book, although not as critical as part 2, has many very valuable chapters.  The performance measurement and management chapters are exceptional providing a framework readers can use to identify performance drivers, develop metrics to assess results, and design compensation structures to reward managers among other things.  For those thinking of purchasing the high-priced Valuation cd-rom, I do not recommend it.  The model included with the fourth edition of the text is much more simplistic than the examples shown in the book.  In short, this book is outstanding.  Every manager should read this book as well as Value: The Four Cornerstones of Corporate Finance, also written by consultants at McKinsey.  If you have to choose just one of the books, choose Valuation.

1596432594|5.0|I first heard about Feynman while I was a Freshman physics major over 40 years ago, when I bought my copies of the Feynman lectures.  Many years later I would read his papers on quantum field theory, attend his lecture on nanotechnology at Bell Labs, and hear from my father-in-law what an inspiring quantum mechanics course Feynman had given at Cornell.  This book is a good introduction to this world for either the layman or young people interested in science--and not just concerning the life of Richard Feynman, but regarding the habits of scientists and engineers in general.  Although most of us in the science and engineering community are (naturally!) not as brilliant as Feynman, our life stories and habits are similar in many respects--from a degree of social cluelessness that becomes painfully evident in our high school years, to arcane interests ranging from obscure ethnic music to dead languages.<br /><br />In addition to relating events in Feynman's life, from his safe cracking adventures while working on the atomic bomb at Los Alamos during WWII, to his reluctant acceptance of the Nobel Prize, the author has the Feymnan character in his cartoon panels explain many aspects of the physics to which he contributed.  I should note that many of these explanations are difficult to follow, and that the reader should not be unduly concerned if some of the points addressed seem illusive.  These difficulties are similar to those which I encountered in reading [[ASIN:0201510057 The Feynman Lectures on Physics: Commemorative Issue, Volume 3: Quantum Mechanics]].  In that volume Feynman often emphasizes fundamental difficulties in understanding rather than focusing on the illuminating successes of quantum theory such as Schrodinger's solution of the hydrogen atom.
0618918248|5.0|The God Delusion  is a well-written, entertaining critique of organized religion and fundamentalism.  Dawkins is clearly a truth seeker, and some of his points are difficult to refute.  For example, he shows how the argument from intelligent design is its own undoing.  If items of great complexity are proof of a designer, that designer himself, being quite complex, must have been designed by an even more talented designer.  The book has two major weaknesses.  First, Dawkins makes no mention of the Sociology of Religion, preferring instead an evolutionary viewpoint.  He asks, \\"If religious beliefs are irrational, how did they survive the process of natural selection?\\"  He then discusses meme theory, meme's being ideas and beliefs whose evolution can be compared to the evolution of living organisms.  Those are interesting ideas.  But there is an entire body of literature, based in the Sociology of Knowledge, that provides better, more logical answers.  Well-known books like The Social Construction of Reality and The Sacred Canopy explain how humans create their own reality, and then anchor this cosmically through religion.  They also explain how reality thus constructed then becomes subjectively real to individuals, and how the human creation of religion in turn acts back upon its human creators.  Dawkins should let us know his position on this.  The second weakness is the lack of appreciation for religion's positive social and personal impacts.  A lot of altruistic behavior, social compassion, sincerity and value clarification are tied up in religion.  Yes, that doesn't prove that God is real. Yes, many evils also occur in the name of religion, such as making children afraid of Hell.  And yes, atheists can have positive values as well.  But it's naïve to believe the religious enterprise should just be stripped away because it's not based on logical argument.    Religion should continue to evolve and modernize.  It should embrace science fully, and let go of beliefsthat contradict science.  The New Testament is an improvement over the Old.  Currently, a big part of Christianity is the personal development angle.  And our view of God changes as cultural values change.  For example, since we no longer believe homosexuals should be executed, be assume the Old Testament was wrong about that.  Obviously, we're invoking moral standards that are independent of religion or at least of the Bible, as Dawkins points out.  The works of Bishop Spong illustrate how far this process has come.  Faith in the absence of proof can be a good thing, and that can only be measured by our ever-evolving values.    Nonetheless, a must read for believers and nonbelievers alike who seek the truth and want to think more clearly.

0385344325|5.0|Major Jack Reacher reporting for duty.  Terrific back-to-the-future Jack Reacher prequel:  Now we know the \\"Rest of the `Reacher' story\\"!  This is one of Lee Child's very best novels and harkens back to his first seven Reacher books in literary style and genius.  Probably the hardest thing to do in fiction literature is to write a good prequel, Mr. Child pulls it off in The Affair.  Even the book is written as a faux prequel of itself as he starts with the end then jumps to the beginning.  Dangerous, but Mr. Child is such an outstanding writer he accomplishes it to perfection.  Well done sir, well done.  The Affair will rank as one of Mr. Child's very best.  Especially after a run of good but not great Reacher efforts.  The Affair has Jack Reacher back in uniform as a Major in the U.S. Army in 1997.  He is detailed to a secret army base in Mississippi to go undercover and investigate the murder of a local woman.  The story develops as he is forced to work with the local sheriff, a former Marine female.  As the tale unfolds nothing is as it appears and soon Reacher discovers that several other local women have been killed over the preceding year.  As an Army CID investigator he becomes conflicted as he must protect the Army while his ethical duty demands he determine the truth no matter where it leads.  The elusive truth is out there but takes Major Reacher to the absolute limits of his investigative abilities to find it.  At times it appears EVERYONE is against Reacher and playing him for their own purposes, but Jack Reacher is smarter than all of them as The Affair unfolds.  Brilliant story wonderfully written.  Bravo Lee Child.  No gratuitous sex, language or violence.  Lots of well written action highlighted with wonderful deductive reasoning.  Superb character development.  This novel is a historical Jack Reacher gold mine.  Reacher is back in the army as a CID army investigator wearing his Class A uniform.  He is not the totally free spirit we have grown accustom to in other Reacher novels.  In The Affair he answers to an Army chain-of-command, and in some cases this chain-of-command is hostile to Reacher as it appears to be hiding an awful secret.  But Reacher's true character shows through, and by the concluding pages we now know why he left his beloved military for the freedom to wander as his own man on the highways and byways of America.  Must read if you are a Jack Reacher devotee and/or like Lee Child.  Mr. Child has written one terrific novel.  I know hardbacks are expensive so you might one to pick up this book at your local library.  It will be very tough to wait for the paperback--this book is just too good.  5+ stars.  Bravo Mr. Child keep up the  great writing.

B000002G7G|4.0|The Velvet Underground (self-titled - 1969)<br /><br />I've loved this band ever since a friend turned me on to them back in '89. I love and appreciate the impact they've had on rock n' roll and I'm an especially big fan of the songs themselves, many of which I consider to be all-time classics. But I've got to get something off my chest - Lou Reed's voice is pretty terrible. Not his voice per se but the fact that he sings flat. I recently listened to this album in the car on a road trip with my mom who has a good ear for music. She thought the songs were good but disliked Reed's voice, saying it was out of tune. She's got a point...still, Reed always puts a lot into his singing and delivers with great style and enthusiasm. I dig his voice, faults and all. Rock n' roll ain't always pretty! And he's a pretty creative vocal stylist: sometimes dark, sometimes funny, sometimes sweet, sometimes full-throttle gonzo, always entertaining. But yeah, technically, his voice sucks.<br /><br />As you've surely noticed by my rating, I don't hold THE VELVET UNDERGROUND in as high esteem as many if not most VU fans. This, their third album, is certainly a radical departure from the sonic meltdowns on their previous album WHITE LIGHT/WHITE HEAT (- admittedly my favorite kind of Velvet music). The band claims their amps and gear were stolen which forced them to make a quieter album, although Lou Reed claims he wanted to go in this direction anyway to show a different side of the band. Most of the album contains top tier Velvet material, but as for the last three songs...well, let's just go straight to the...<br /><br />Breakdown!<br /><br />\\"Candy Says\\" (4:02) - While most of the songs are sung by Reed, it's new bassist Doug Yule who takes the vocals on the opening track. I think his voice sounds really nice here. His gentle, high-pitched singing compliments the androgynous nature of the song which concerns a real-life transvestite named Candy Darling. Such a quiet song, and that makes it a rather daring way to begin the album. \\"What do you think I'd see/ If I could walk away from me?\\" I think this is one of their best songs. *****+<br /><br />\\"What Goes On\\" (4:52) - The album instantly bursts to life the moment this infectious groover starts up. The clanging guitars really drive the song and when the guitar solo kicks in it sounds smooth as silk (note - see discussion in comments section regarding which Velvet actually performs this solo!). It's a really upbeat, feel-good song and it lasts a good long time too. I love hearing the bootlegs where it goes on forever - I never get tired of hearing it! *****+<br /><br />\\"Some Kinda Love\\" (4:00) - This one brings a real twangy country flavor, accentuated by drummer Mo Tucker on cowbell. It moves at a steady pace and a lot of the fun comes from hearing Reed strangely enunciating his intriguing and quite baffling lyrics. *****<br /><br />\\"Pale Blue Eyes\\" (5:38) - is considered a top classic among Velvet fans and no doubt it is gorgeous. It's slow and sweet if lyrically simplistic in a few places. But the lilting guitars are lovely and Mo's gentle tambourine is a nice touch. It's not a personal favorite of mine, but I'll give it five stars because it truly deserves it. It never wears out its welcome. *****<br /><br />\\"Jesus\\" (3:22) - Funny that I love this heart-felt prayer to Jesus Christ - I'm an atheist. But I find the song to be truly compelling and sweetly sung. The guitars are pristine, serene and simple - a beautiful song! *****<br /><br />\\"Beginning To See The Light\\" (4:38) - This is another jubilant feel-good song, the kind VU were so great at making. I would say this is one of their best in that vein. Reed is really funny on this one, just off the chain. This is classic VU - really upbeat, good-time rock music. *****+<br /><br />\\"I'm Set Free\\" (4:01) - is my favorite track on THE VELVET UNDERGROUND and I truly would've been happy if the record ended here, short as it would have been (and really, too short for an album, especially in 1969...but the rules were eventually bent. For example, the B-52's released a cool six song mini-album called MESOPOTAMIA in 1982 - too short to be an lp, too long to be an ep...). Anyway, \\"I'm Set Free\\" is simply majestic. Lou Reed rejoices in his new found freedom both solemnly and triumphantly. As to what he's been set free from, that's up to the listener. It's a very uplifting song until Reed throws you a curve ball: \\"I'm set free - to find a new illusion\\". I consider this song to be one of their best; it's definitely one of my personal favs. Certainly it ranks high among fans and I say it would've been the perfect way to close the album (in fact, I typically program out the last three songs.)*****+<br /><br />\\"That's The Story of My Life\\" (1:56) - This is an extremely dorky, rinkydink tune that I just can't stand and always skip. I don't think it's a good song at all and I find it really annoying. At least it's really short. *<br /><br />\\"The Murder Mystery\\" (8:53) - The weird one. Count on the Velvets to throw something bizarre into the mix. While I love experimental music, I don't often put this one on. It's definitely interesting and worth hearing - one or two times at least. I especially love it when this strangely oscillating organ comes in; it's sinister and intense as is the insistent guitar line. Taking avant garde to a new and different extreme, Lou Reed and guitarist Sterling Morrison recite words (some might say poetry), one guy regulated to each stereo speaker. They both talk at the same time and, disconcertingly, at different speeds. It's quite strange and it does sound kinda cool. But these segments are periodically interrupted by this limp, cheesy music and terribly off-key singing from both drummer Maureen Tucker and Doug Yule (who sounds awful here despite his lovely performance on \\"Candy Says\\"). The track eventually morphs into a bizarre finale with a climbing piano figure and various sound effects including what I'm pretty sure is a backwards guitar. All the poetry they throw at you sounds random and meaningless although the images are fascinating and occasionally thought provoking. The whole thing has this old TV detective vibe and the atmosphere is palpable. But because of Mo and Doug's singing, I unfortunately just can't bear to hear this track...I've actually thought about making a version that excises the offending parts and keeps the cool stuff. I would definitely listen to that particular version if I could pull it off. (ha ha thinking back there was one thing in particular that Mo would sing - \\"Oboe and me\\" (ugh) - that used to irritate me in the extreme. I hated it so much that if it was about to come on I would run across the room to stop it from playing!) ***<br /><br />\\"After Hours\\" (2:07) - Lou Reed may think it's charming but I really can't honestly say I like Mo Tucker's voice. It's small, cute, and sweet but her singing is hopelessly off key. However, the song itself is quite charming. It's got a quaint sound with a jazzy guitar strumming along to a bumbling bass and, OK, it is endearing to hear Mo long for a love in her life, one who will make the night \\"last forever\\". I often skip this song, but I'll be generous and give it four stars to acknowledge the legitimate appeal it has for some. ****<br /><br />note: There are two different mixes of this album. Personally I don't think it makes a whole lot of difference which copy you get. It used to be that one would almost certainly get the \\"rock mix\\" when buying this CD which was the original version released - against Lou's wishes. His own \\"closet mix\\" is the one he wanted to put out. It basically just boosts his vocals higher in the mix and it's long been available on the PEEL SLOWLY AND SEE box set. With the closet mix, Lou was trying to create a certain intimate atmosphere on the album and I would say the new remaster of THE VELVET UNDERGROUND pretty much achieves his original vision. As for myself, I can appreciate all these different versions. They're all good in their own way, and it's fun to decide for yourself which one you actually prefer.<br /><br />Despite the four-star rating, this album is highly recommended.uding what I'm pretty sure is a backwards guitar. B005LCECU6|4.0|When I heard about Rush releasing the Time Machine DVD (which I finally got last week with a gift card), I wasn't exactly in a hurry or rush to run out and buy it. Why? Because it seems like Rush have now been releasing a live DVD and/or CD for every current tour. And how many live versions of Tom Sawyer and Closer To The Heart do we need?<br /><br />When I heard about THIS CD, however, I immediately knew I had to have it and bought it almost immediately. The early period of Rush had so far been represented by just one live album, All The World's A Stage. This release goes back 2 years earlier, to 1974, while Rush were on tour for their first album and within a month or two of Neil Peart joining the group. This was a very young and loud and heavy and slightly obnoxious, almost punk-rock like in their energy, guitar driven Rush. And damn is it fun to listen to! Tis a pleasure to hear Geddy singing in his original granny, screeching impossibly high voice (even though towards the end you may...like me...find yourself nearly missing that smoother lower and mid range vocals of his from upcoming albums). It's cool hearing pre-Fly By Night versions of 'Best I Can' (here listed as 'THE Best I Can') and 'In The End'. And it's also wonderful to hear that original ultra thick heavy metal guitar sound of Lifeson's (that I missed so much during their synthesizer heavy Power Windows era). Early Rush fans will eat this up, partly due to the abundance of songs never heard on a Rush live album before, like 'Here Again', 'Best I Can' and 'Need Some Love' (okay, live versions of these last two do appear on some Rush DVDs, but I'm not really counting that) and for songs never heard on ANY Rush album before, like 'Bad Boy', 'Fancy Dancer' and 'Garden Road'. I would have to say my favorite moment on the whole CD is the intro for 'Need Some Love', which starts off with what sounds like to me some Ramones-like guitar riffing. Very cool!<br /><br />In terms of the mix, this is relatively well recorded, especially seeing that very little post-production work was done on it...or at least it seems that way. One can hear all the instruments very clearly, the guitar is very loud in the mix (as are Peart's drums)and Geddy's bass is also much more prominent in the mix than it was on any of the early Rush albums. The only time the sound bothers me is on the three bonus tracks from 1975, which show up at the end of the album. The bass is far too loud in the mix and very muddy sounding, and the guitar is almost completely buried beneath the bass and drums. While it's nice to hear these three songs again ('Anthem' , 'Beneath, Between & Behind' and 'Fly By Night'), I feel like this may have been a better album without them, due to their lousy sound.<br /><br />Overall, I would consider this a must have for early Rush fans, moreso than the recent Time Machine CD/DVD, at least from a historical point of view and to hear Rush back when they were still a humble heavy metal trio, before they got progressive and scientific. (And yes, I do like their progressive and scientific side!) I would give this 4 and a half stars. It would be a 5 if not for the bonus tracks. 0525478817|5.0|I always approach a new book with a sense of trepidation, particularly when the author is one that I am fond of. There's always the fear that this new book won't live up to my expectations, and then what? And, for the first chapter or so, this concern was not completely washed away. Sure, it was good, but was it ~really~ going to be better than Looking for Alaska, the way it had been hyped up to be? It didn't seem so, at first.   Still, it wasn't long before the writing settled into place and the characters began to take on sympathetic lives. I think John Green did what he set out to do--create a book about cancer, but not about cancer; write a story where the characters were not glorified as noble sufferers, but as people who are just as scared and lonely as anyone would be in their place. True to fashion, he wove humor amongst even the darker hours; he presented metaphors that were perhaps not in the grand literary fashion, but were honest to the human condition; he reminded readers that perhaps our heroes are not all we have idealized them to be.   I admit. I cried. I cried hard, but not for the characters in the book---I cried because the story found within it spoke to the reality of life; the reality that this could happen even to the person I care about most. I would confidently recommend this book to anyone, young or old, as a read that will engage both mind and emotion on a journey that leaves you not with a sense of loss, but rather a feeling of personal gain for having read it.

B002MQYOFW|5.0|Hunger Games  -Suzanne Collins  A best-selling YA book, certainly a must read for me. Though I had to repeat my \\"Please Please\\" slogans for getting this book, it was worth the money and the effort. Super easy to understand prose style of writing, and an extremely beautifully designed plot, this book often sent `Chills down my spine'. The story is made in the not very distant future where the whole of North America is made into a country (or continent rather) called \\"Panem\\", consisting of thirteen districts, and the \\"Capitol\\". All thirteen of these districts held a rebellion against the Capitol  in which the thirteenth was destroyed. So now, each year, the Capitol holds the Hunger Games in which 2 \\"Tributes\\" one girl and one boy is selected from each of the twelve districts who then fight till the death in an arena. The last man standing is declared the winner, and his/her district and the person himself is awarded enormously. The story revolves around a sixteen year old girl named \\"Katniss Everdeen\\" and a boy \\"Peeta Mellark\\" who are selected from their district, District 12, to participate in the Hunger Games fight against children younger than them and some tributes who are killing machines and who have trained themselves their whole life for this event. Breath-taking, full of adventure, unexpected twists, and of course fight. Suzanne Collins has done a mind-blowing job constructing the characters personalities and the cruelty of the Games. Being a YA book, this book is suitable for 12 and above.  With number 1 ranking in the Amazon Kindle Store, and in the top 100 for about 600+ days, this is a must read.  \\"May the odds be ever in your favour\\"
